import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers=3, bias=True, batch_first=False):
        """
        :param input_size: The number of expected features in the input x
        :param hidden_size: The number of features in the hidden state h
        :param num_layers: Number of recurrent layers (default: 1)
        :param bias: If False, then the layer does not use bias weights b_ih and b_hh (default: True)
        :param batch_first: If True, then the input and output tensors are provided as (batch, seq, feature) (default: False)
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        self.gru = nn.GRU(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, bias, batch_first, dropout=0, bidirectional=False)
    
    def forward(self, x,h0):
        """
        :param x: The input tensor, shape (seq_len, batch_size, input_size) if batch_first=False, otherwise (batch_size, seq_len, input_size)
        :param h_0: The initial hidden state for the input sequence, shape (num_layers * num_directions, batch_size, hidden_size) (default: None)
        :return: output, h_n
            - output: The output features (h_t) from the last layer of the GRU, for each t, shape (seq_len, batch_size, num_directions * hidden_size) if batch_first=False, otherwise (batch_size, seq_len, num_directions * hidden_size)
            - h_n: The hidden state for t = seq_len, shape (num_layers * num_directions, batch_size, hidden_size)
        """
        output, h_n = self.gru(x, h0)
        return output

# Test code
batch_size = 10
seq_len = 512
input_size = 128
hidden_size = 256
num_layers = 6

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(seq_len, batch_size, input_size),torch.rand((num_layers, batch_size, hidden_size))]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [input_size, hidden_size, num_layers]

The task is to replace a PyTorch GRU with a custom CUDA kernel. The GPU is an H100 with huge compute (1979 TFLOPS BF16/FP16). A GRU layer in LSTM style has the gates: r_t = sigmoid(W_ir x_t + b_ir + W_hr h_{t-1} + b_hr) z_t = sigmoid(W_iz x_t + b_iz + W_hz h_{t-1} + b_hz) n_t = tanh(W_in x_t + b_in + r_t * (W_hn h_{t-1} + b_hn)) h_t = (1 - z_t) * n_t + z_t * h_{t-1} PyTorch GRU uses this default (unless reset_after=True, but for `nn.GRU` default is reset_after=False? Actually in PyTorch, nn.GRU uses the formula: r_t = sigmoid(W_ir x_t + b_ir + W_hr h_{t-1} + b_hr) z_t = sigmoid(W_iz x_t + b_iz + W_hz h_{t-1} + b_hz) n_t = tanh(W_in x_t + b_in + r_t * (W_hn h_{t-1} + b_hn)) h_t = (1 - z_t) * n_t + z_t * h_{t-1} Wait, PyTorch's `nn.GRU` uses `reset_after=False`? Let's check. Actually, from PyTorch docs: ``` \hat{h}_t = \tanh(W_{ih} x_t + b_{ih} + r_t * (W_{hh} h_{t-1} + b_{hh})) h_t = (1 - z_t) * \hat{h}_t + z_t * h_{t-1} ``` This is the `reset_after=False` from cuDNN / standard PyTorch. The weights shape is `(3*hidden_size, input_size)` for weight_ih and `(3*hidden_size, hidden_size)` for weight_hh. The biases are `(3*hidden_size)`. For `num_layers` GRU, the first layer takes input_size -> hidden_size, subsequent layers take hidden_size -> hidden_size. Now, to implement a fast GRU kernel, I have several options: 1. Use a fused kernel per layer, computing the recurrence with each thread handling a batch element and hidden dimension, etc. 2. Precompute the input contributions `W_ih x_t + b_ih` for all time steps, then do the recurrence with hidden weights. Given the sizes: batch=10, seq_len=512, input_size=128, hidden_size=256, num_layers=6. This is relatively small batch. H100 has massive compute, but memory bandwidth might be the bottleneck if we do too many separate kernel launches. Total FLOPs per layer per step: ~ 2 * input_size * hidden_size + 4 * hidden_size * hidden_size (since weight_ih is 3*hidden_size x input_size = 3* in*hid, weight_hh is 3*hidden x hidden = 3*hid*hid). For the first layer: 3*(128*256) + 3*(256*256) = 3*(32768+65536) = 3*98304 = 294912 multiply-adds per step per batch. With batch=10, seq=512: ~ 1.5 billion FLOPs per layer. 6 layers -> ~9 billion FLOPs. FP32 on H100 is 67 TFLOPS, so compute bound is ~0.13 ms. DRAM bandwidth is 3.35 TB/s. Weights per first layer: 3*128*256 + 3*256*256 = 294912 floats = 1.18 MB. For 6 layers: ~ (layer 1: 1.18 MB, layers 2-6: 5 * (3*256*256 + 3*256*256) = 5 * 393216 = 1,966,080 floats = 7.86 MB). Total weights ~9 MB. Activations per step per layer: x (128*10), h (256*10), plus intermediate. Data movement maybe small. But if we launch many small kernels, overhead may dominate. PyTorch's cuDNN GRU is heavily optimized, possibly using persistent kernels or fused kernels. On small batch, it may be memory/launch bound. To beat it, we need a very efficient fused implementation. One approach: - Use torch.matmul to compute all `W_ih @ x_t` for all t in parallel. - Then fused kernel for recurrence using precomputed input projections. For each layer: 1. `igate = x @ W_ih.T + b_ih` shape (seq, batch, 3*hidden) 2. For each t in 0..seq-1: - compute `hgate = h_prev @ W_hh.T` -> (batch, 3*hidden) - split into r, z, n1 (for reset gate applied to hidden) - Actually `hgate` is (b, 3*hidden). Then: r = sigmoid(igate_r + hgate_r + b_hr) z = sigmoid(igate_z + hgate_z + b_hz) n_tanh_input = igate_n + b_hn + r * hgate_n n = tanh(n_tanh_input) h = (1 - z) * n + z * h_prev Wait, the bias: `b_ih` and `b_hh`. So we can pre-add `b_ih` to `igate` and add `b_hh` to `hgate`. Or we can fuse biases into the weights if we add a constant 1 to input, but easiest is pre-add during projection. PyTorch stores weights as `weight_ih_l{k}` shape `(3*hidden_size, input_size)` and `weight_hh_l{k}` shape `(3*hidden_size, hidden_size)`. Biases are `bias_ih_l{k}` and `bias_hh_l{k}` shape `(3*hidden_size)`. So for each layer: - Compute `proj_x = F.linear(x, weight_ih, bias_ih)` -> (seq, batch, 3*hidden) if x is (seq, batch, input) - Then iterate t: - `proj_h = F.linear(h_prev, weight_hh, bias_hh)` -> (batch, 3*hidden) - unpack. - compute gates. Since seq_len=512 and batch=10, iterating in Python would be slow (512 steps). We need a fused CUDA kernel that iterates over t. Kernel design for a single layer: - Launch 1D blocks over batch elements (or maybe 2D over batch and hidden chunks). - Each block processes one batch element (or a small group) and all hidden units. - The block loads `weight_hh` into shared memory? `weight_hh` is `(3*hidden, hidden)` = 768*256 = 196,608 floats = 768 KB. This fits in shared memory (227 KB per block max) wait: 768 KB > 227 KB. So cannot load full weight matrix. - Need to tile. Each block can process a chunk of hidden units, say `hidden_per_block = 64`. Then weight needed: for computing output for 64 hidden units, we need `weight_hh[chunk:chunk+64, :]` shape (64, hidden) = 64*256 = 16K floats = 64 KB. Plus `weight_ih`? No, input projection is done before kernel, so only hidden weight and biases. Alternatively, process multiple batch elements per block to amortize weight loading. For example, a block of 256 threads processes a chunk of 64 hidden units for 4 batch elements sequentially? Or use one warp per batch element. Simpler approach: Use thread-level matmul for small batch. Since hidden_size=256 and batch=10, we can have each block handle one time step for all batch elements, but that's not efficient due to recurrence. Better: Use CUTLASS-like persistent kernel or just PyTorch's `torch.addmm` for each step. However, Python loop over 512 steps will be very slow (kernel launch overhead per step). We need to fuse the entire sequence. A good strategy for small batch GRU: 1. Precompute `Wx = x @ W_ih.T + b_ih`. Since x is (seq, batch, input), we can reshape to (seq*batch, input) and do one `addmm`: `(seq*batch, 3*hidden)`. 2. Then have a CUDA kernel that loops over t=0..seq-1. Each thread block handles a subset of the hidden dimension and a subset of the batch dimension. For instance, we can launch blocks with dimensions `(batch, ceil(hidden / H))`. Each block processes one batch element and a chunk of `H` hidden features across all time steps. Within the block, threads collectively load `weight_hh` for their chunk. But each chunk needs `weight_hh[chunk, :]` (size H * hidden). We can keep the weight in registers or shared memory. If H=64, weight slice is 64*256*4 = 64KB. This fits in shared memory (max 227KB). We can load it once per block. Bias is tiny. Then for each time step t: - We have `h_prev` for the batch element, all hidden. We need to compute `gate = weight_hh[chunk, :] @ h_prev + bias_hh[chunk]`. This is a matrix-vector product: (H, hidden) @ (hidden,) -> (H,). - We can parallelize this across the block: each thread computes a few elements of the gate. We can store `h_prev` in shared memory (256 floats = 1KB). Then each thread computes dot product of a row of weight_hh with h_prev. Since H=64 and block has e.g. 256 threads, we can assign 1 row to 4 threads or 4 rows per thread. - After computing gate chunk, add precomputed `Wx[t, batch, chunk]` (loaded from global memory), compute r, z, n, and update h. - Store updated h chunk to global memory (or shared memory for next step). This approach is very efficient: one kernel launch per layer, weight slice loaded once per block, sequential over time steps but fully parallel within each step for the matvec. Given batch=10, hidden=256. Let's set `H = 64`. Then number of blocks = batch * (hidden / H) = 10 * 4 = 40 blocks. H100 has many SMs (e.g., 132 SMs). 40 blocks is not enough to saturate all SMs. But each block does 512 sequential steps, so occupancy may be okay if they run fast. Still, 40 blocks is a bit low. We can increase parallelism by processing e.g. `H=32` -> 10 * 8 = 80 blocks. Or `H=16` -> 160 blocks. But smaller H means less work per block and more overhead loading weights. Let's see. Alternatively, each block can process multiple batch elements. For example, block processes 2 batch elements and H=64 hidden chunk. Then blocks = 5 * 4 = 20. Even worse. Better: use 2D grid where blockDim.x handles the hidden dimension (e.g. 32 or 64 threads) and blockDim.y handles the batch dimension (e.g. 1 or more). Each block handles one time step? No, we want to loop over time inside kernel to avoid global synchronization. What if we make each warp process one batch element, and block has multiple warps (e.g. 4 warps = 128 threads) processing 4 batch elements. But the matvec for hidden weights can be done with warp-level reductions. Let's think about the size. hidden=256. We can have each thread compute one hidden unit for one batch element. That is 10*256 = 2560 threads total. If we launch 2560 threads, they will be distributed across SMs. But they need to loop over time steps. The problem is computing `weight_hh @ h_prev`. If each thread computes one output element, it needs to compute dot product of a row of weight_hh (length 256) with h_prev (length 256). That's 256 multiply-adds per thread per step. For 512 steps, 131k FLOPs per thread. With 2560 threads, total FLOPs ~ 336M for the hidden matmul (but actual is 3*hidden*hidden*seq*batch = 3*256*256*512*10 = 1B). Wait, if each thread computes only one gate component (there are 3*hidden gates), we need 768 outputs per batch per step. So 10*768 = 7680 threads. Each thread does a dot product of length 256. That's 7680*256 = 1.97M multiply-adds per step. Over 512 steps -> 1B FLOPs, correct. Can we launch 7680 threads? Yes. Group them into blocks of e.g. 256 threads -> 30 blocks. Each thread computes one gate element for one batch element. But each thread needs to read 256 weights and 256 h_prev values. That's a lot of global memory reads if not cached. However, `h_prev` is shared across all threads in a block (for the same batch element). `weight_hh` rows are unique per thread (or per a few threads). H100 L2 cache is large (50MB), weight_hh is ~0.75MB per layer, so weights might stay in L2. But each step, each thread reads 256 floats from `h_prev`, which is 256*4=1KB per batch element. With 30 blocks, total h_prev reads per step = 7680*256*4 = 7.5MB. Over 512 steps = 3.8GB. H100 memory bandwidth is huge, but still. What about registers? Each thread needs to accumulate a dot product of 256 elements. That's 256 loads + 256 FMAs. This is straightforward. The main cost is memory traffic for `h_prev` (shared) and weights (cached). But we need to ensure coalescing: each thread reads a row of `weight_hh`. If we store `weight_hh` row-major (shape (3*hidden, hidden)), consecutive threads (in x) reading consecutive rows will have stride hidden*4 = 1024 bytes, which is coalesced if threads are reading contiguous addresses? Actually, if thread i reads row i, the first element of row i is at address i*hidden*4. Consecutive threads read addresses with stride hidden*4, which is not coalesced. This is bad for global memory. We should transpose `weight_hh` to column-major or store as (hidden, 3*hidden) so that consecutive threads read within the same column (i.e., same offset in h_prev). Wait, no: for dot product `row @ h_prev`, each thread reads consecutive elements along the row. If rows are stored contiguously, thread i and thread i+1 read elements at `base + i*hidden + k` and `base + (i+1)*hidden + k` for same k. That's strided by hidden, not contiguous. Coalescing requires consecutive threads read consecutive memory locations. So for dot product at index k, we want thread i to read `weight[k, i]` if thread i handles output i. That means weight should be stored transposed (shape (hidden, 3*hidden)). So we should transpose `weight_hh` to `(hidden, 3*hidden)` before passing to kernel, and compute dot product as `sum_k weight_t[k, gid] * h_prev[k]`. Then at step k, threads gid, gid+1, ... read `weight_t[k, gid]`, `weight_t[k, gid+1]`, which are contiguous. Perfect coalescing. Similarly for `weight_ih`, we precompute `Wx = x @ weight_ih.T`. This is a standard PyTorch matmul and stores output as (seq, batch, 3*hidden). We don't need to transpose weight_ih in the kernel. So for the recurrence kernel: - Transpose `weight_hh` to `(hidden, 3*hidden)` on CPU before launching. - `h` is (batch, hidden). We'll update it in place or use ping-pong buffers. Since we need `h_n` for next layer? Wait, for multi-layer GRU, the output of layer l at time t is the input of layer l+1 at time t. But PyTorch GRU with num_layers processes all time steps of layer 1, then layer 2, etc. Actually for standard RNNs, it's layer-wise: layer 1 processes the whole sequence, producing output sequence of shape (seq, batch, hidden). That becomes input to layer 2, and so on. This matches the PyTorch implementation. So we can process layers sequentially. For each layer: 1. Compute `Wx` = `x @ W_ih.T + b_ih`. `x` shape: (seq, batch, input_size). Output: (seq, batch, 3*hidden). This is one big gemm + bias. 2. Run fused kernel over `Wx`, `W_hh`, `b_hh`, `h0[layer]`, producing `output` (seq, batch, hidden) and updated `h` for the layer (batch, hidden). The kernel loops over seq. This is 6 layers, each with one gemm + one fused kernel. The gemm can be done efficiently with cuBLAS. Is this faster than cuDNN? cuDNN likely fuses the GEMM and recurrence, and uses matrix splitting or wavefront algorithms. For small batch (10), cuDNN may not be fully optimized because it focuses on larger batch. A custom kernel that does exactly the small-batch optimized recurrence might win. But the overhead of 6 separate gemm + kernel launches may add up. Let's estimate time for gemm: (seq*batch, 3*hidden) = (5120, 768) @ (input_size, ...) Actually `x` is (seq, batch, input) reshaped to (seq*batch, input). `W_ih` is (3*hidden, input). So gemm is `(seq*batch, input) @ (input, 3*hidden).T` -> m=5120, k=input (128 or 256), n=3*hidden (768). flop = 2*m*k*n. Layer 1: m=5120, k=128, n=768 -> 2*5120*128*768 = 1.007B FLOPs. Layer 2-6: m=5120, k=256, n=768 -> 2*5120*256*768 = 2.014B FLOPs per layer. Total gemm FLOPs ~ 1 + 5*2 = 11B FLOPs. Recurrence FLOPs: per step per batch: 3*hidden*hidden = 3*256*256 = 196608 for hgate, plus elementwise ops. For seq=512, batch=10, 6 layers: 196608 * 512 * 10 * 6 = 6.03B FLOPs. Total FLOPs ~ 17B. At 67 TFLOPS FP32, this is ~0.25 ms. In practice, kernels will take longer due to memory traffic and overhead. cuDNN GRU on this size might take around 0.5-1ms? Let's see. Actually cuDNN is very fast. On A100, a GRU with similar parameters and batch=32 might be ~0.2ms. For batch=10, maybe 0.1ms? We need to beat it. The fact that we can do a direct matmul for input projection (very efficient in cuBLAS) and a custom fused recurrence kernel might be competitive. However, there is a major issue: we also need to compute the output for all time steps. The kernel loops over time sequentially and writes output for each step. This is fine. Let's design the kernel in detail. Kernel signature: ```cpp __global__ void gru_forward( const float* __restrict__ Wx, // (seq, batch, 3*hidden) const float* __restrict__ W_hh_t, // (hidden, 3*hidden) - transposed const float* __restrict__ b_hh, // (3*hidden) const float* __restrict__ h0, // (batch, hidden) float* __restrict__ out, // (seq, batch, hidden) float* __restrict__ h_final, // (batch, hidden) int seq_len, int batch_size, int hidden_size) ``` Grid: `(batch_size, (3*hidden_size + blockDim.x - 1)/blockDim.x)`? Wait, each thread handles one element of the 3*hidden gates? But the recurrence requires computing all 3*hidden gates to then compute r, z, n and update h. We need to keep h in registers/shared across the block. If we assign each thread to one hidden unit (not gate), then each thread needs r, z, n for that hidden unit. But to compute r and z, it only needs the corresponding gate values (one element each). To compute n, it needs the n gate value and also r * (W_hn @ h_prev). Wait, `n_t = tanh(W_in x_t + b_in + r_t * (W_hn h_{t-1} + b_hn))`. The hgate projection is `h_proj = h_prev @ W_hh.T + b_hh`. This produces a vector of length 3*hidden. Let's denote: `hr = h_proj[0:hidden]` `hz = h_proj[hidden:2*hidden]` `hn = h_proj[2*hidden:3*hidden]` Then: `r = sigmoid(Wx_r[t] + hr)` `z = sigmoid(Wx_z[t] + hz)` `n = tanh(Wx_n[t] + r * hn)` // note: no extra bias here since b_hh already added `h_new = (1 - z) * n + z * h_prev` So if each thread handles one hidden index `j` (0..hidden-1), it needs to compute: `hr_j = sum_k W_hh_t[k, j] * h_prev[k] + b_hh[j]` `hz_j = sum_k W_hh_t[k, j + hidden] * h_prev[k] + b_hh[j + hidden]` `hn_j = sum_k W_hh_t[k, j + 2*hidden] * h_prev[k] + b_hh[j + 2*hidden]` So each thread needs to compute 3 dot products with the same `h_prev` but different columns of `W_hh_t`. We can fuse these into a single loop over k. Each thread computes three accumulators. Layout of `W_hh_t`: shape `(hidden, 3*hidden)`, column-major means columns are contiguous. So for thread `j`, it reads `W_hh_t[k, j]`, `W_hh_t[k, j+hidden]`, `W_hh_t[k, j+2*hidden]`. For a fixed k, these are at strides `hidden` elements? Wait, if shape is (hidden, 3*hidden) and stored row-major (which is standard for PyTorch/CUDA), then `W_hh_t[k, c]` is at index `k * (3*hidden) + c`. So for thread j, the addresses are `k*3H + j`, `k*3H + j+H`, `k*3H + j+2H`. For consecutive threads j and j+1, they read `k*3H + j`, `k*3H + j+1`, etc. So for fixed k, consecutive threads read consecutive memory! This is fully coalesced. Perfect. So we can store `W_hh_t` as just the transpose of `W_hh` (i.e., `W_hh_t = W_hh.T`), shape `(hidden, 3*hidden)`. Then each block can process some number of hidden units (say `H_PER_BLOCK`). Let `blockDim.x = H_PER_BLOCK`. Each thread loops over k from 0 to hidden-1, reading `W_hh_t[k, j]`, `W_hh_t[k, j+H]`, `W_hh_t[k, j+2H]` and `h_prev[k]` (shared). It accumulates `acc_r, acc_z, acc_n`. After the loop, add biases. Then load `Wx` for the current time step: `Wx[t, batch, j]`, `Wx[t, batch, j+H]`, `Wx[t, batch, j+2H]`. Compute gates: `r = sigmoid(acc_r + Wx_r)`, `z = sigmoid(acc_z + Wx_z)`, `n = tanh(acc_n + r * Wx_n + r * b_hh_n?)` Wait, bias already added to acc_n. So `n = tanh(acc_n + r * Wx_n)`? Let's recompute. Actually `Wx` includes `b_ih`. `acc_n` includes `b_hh_n`. The formula is: `n_t = tanh(W_in x_t + b_in + r_t * (W_hn h_{t-1} + b_hn))` So `acc_n` = `W_hn h_{t-1} + b_hn`. Then `n = tanh(Wx_n + r * acc_n)`. Where `Wx_n` is `W_in x_t + b_in`. Yes. So `acc_n` is computed with bias. Write output1[0]. - Then compute layer 2 from output1[0] and h0[l=1]. Write output2[0]. - ... - At t=1: compute layer 1 from x[1] and h1[l=0] (updated). Write output1[1]. - Then layer 2 from output1[1] and h1[l=1]. ... This requires that within a block, after computing layer l for time t, the result is available to compute layer l+1. If each block processes the entire hidden vector (all hidden units), we can compute the entire output for one (t, batch) across all layers inside one block. We need to store output1[t] in shared or registers while we compute layer 2, etc. This is great! We can fuse all layers into a single kernel! Let's design this: - We allocate output buffers for each layer? For layer l, we need output[l][t] for the next layer at the same t. But we don't need to store all time steps for intermediate layers if we process layer by layer. Wait, if we process t sequentially and for each t process all layers, we need output of layer l at time t to feed layer l+1 at time t. We can keep it in registers/shared memory for the current t. Then we move to t+1 and the process repeats. We only need to store the final output (layer num_layers) for each t, because that's the model output. But we also need to store the final hidden states h_n for each layer (return value? Actually reference returns only output, but we need h_n for the next step? Wait, h_n is needed to compute the next time step. Inside the kernel that loops over t, we naturally maintain h_prev per layer across t. So we need to carry h_prev for each layer from t to t+1. That's num_layers * hidden floats per batch element. For num_layers=6, hidden=256, that's 1536 floats = 6KB per batch element. We can store this in shared memory per block if block handles one batch element. Or in registers if partitioned across threads. So here's the plan for a single fully-fused kernel: - Launch configuration: one block per batch element. blockDim.x = hidden_size = 256 (or 128, etc.). Since batch=10, we have 10 blocks. - Each block processes all time steps for all layers for that batch element. - Shared memory: - `s_h[l][j]`: hidden state for each layer l (num_layers=6) and hidden unit j. Size = 6*256*4 = 6KB. - `s_out[j]`: temporary output for current layer before passing to next layer. Size = 256*4 = 1KB. - Alternatively, we can compute layer l and write to `s_out`, then use `s_out` as input to layer l+1. But we need to keep `s_h` for each layer updated. - Registers per thread: each thread can handle one or more hidden units. Let's say blockDim.x = 256 threads. Each thread handles one hidden unit j. The kernel loops over t = 0..seq-1: - Read x_t = x[t, batch, :] (input_size floats). Since input_size=128, we need to load it into shared memory or registers. Threads 0..127 load x_t[k] into shared `s_x[k]`. threads 128..255 do nothing or help. - For layer = 0..num_layers-1: - Determine input vector for this layer: - If layer == 0: input is s_x (length input_size=128). - Else: input is s_out from previous layer (length hidden_size=256). Actually after layer 0, s_out contains the output of layer 0 (a hidden vector). We can alias s_x and s_out? We can have `s_in` of size hidden (256). For layer 0, we only use first input_size elements. - Compute input projection: for this thread j (0..255): - `acc_r = b_ih[layer][j]`, etc. - Loop k over input_size (or hidden_size), accumulate. - Compute hidden projection: for this thread j: - `acc_hr = 0`, loop k over hidden_size, accumulate `W_hh_t[layer][k, j] * s_h[layer][k]`. - Compute gates and new h. - Write new h to `s_h[layer][j]` and `s_out[j]`. - __syncthreads(). - Write s_out (which is output of last layer) to output[t, batch, j]. Wait, after each layer, we need a __syncthreads because the next layer needs `s_out` to be fully written (if next layer uses it as input). Also, for the hidden projection of the same layer, we need `s_h[layer]` to be fully updated before the next time step. Since all threads in the block participate, __syncthreads is needed after each layer and after each time step? Let's see. Inside the layer loop: - `s_h[layer]` is read for hidden projection. We need it to be fully written from previous time step. At the start of t=0, it's initialized from h0. After each layer at time t, we write to `s_h[layer][j]`. Then __syncthreads to ensure it's visible for the next time step. - `s_out` is written by all threads. Then __syncthreads before next layer reads it. So layer loop: ``` for (int l=0; l<num_layers; ++l) { // compute projections using s_h[l] and input // compute h_new s_h[l][j] = h_new; if (l < num_layers-1) s_out[j] = h_new; __syncthreads(); } ``` After the layer loop, write final output: ``` out[t * batch * hidden + batch * hidden + j] = s_h[num_layers-1][j]; // or s_out ``` But note: `s_out` after layer num_layers-1 is the final output. We could reuse `s_h[num_layers-1]` as final output, but we updated it. So after the loop, `s_h[num_layers-1]` is the output for time t. We can write that. Wait, but `s_h[num_layers-1]` is also the hidden state for layer num_layers-1 to be used at time t+1. So after writing output, we don't need an extra sync before next time step because the next time step's layer 0 reads `s_h[0]`, not `s_h[num_layers-1]`. However, after the last layer's __syncthreads, all `s_h[l]` are up to date for time t. Then we proceed to t+1. Before reading `s_h[l]` for any l, we are already synchronized because all threads reached the end of the for-t loop body. So the `t` loop naturally has a sync at the end of each iteration (implicit after layer loop). Actually no: threads might diverge if there are conditionals, but they all execute the same path. After the inner layer loop, all threads hit __syncthreads num_layers times. So at the start of the next t, `s_h` is consistent. However, __syncthreads inside a loop over layers may be expensive. num_layers=6, seq=512: 3072 barriers. With 10 blocks, each block has 256 threads (8 warps). 3072 barriers * 10 blocks on maybe 10 SMs. Each barrier is a few cycles if all warps are resident. With 8 warps per SM, it's okay. But wait: 10 blocks on H100 may be mapped to 10 different SMs. Each SM has 8 warps = 256 threads. That's a decent amount. H100 SM can hold up to 64 warps. So each SM could run more blocks if available. But we only have 10 blocks. So 10 SMs are active, each with 8 warps. 8 warps may not fully saturate the SM's compute units (tensor cores), but it's okay. The real question: is this kernel faster than cuDNN? The advantage of a single kernel for all layers is massive: - Only one kernel launch instead of 6. - No intermediate global memory writes for layer outputs except the final output buffer. - We only read x (seq, batch, input) and write out (seq, batch, hidden). Plus weights and biases. - Global memory traffic is minimized to the theoretical minimum. - We avoid the overhead of 6 cuBLAS calls. This is a strong candidate to beat cuDNN. But we need to handle weights and biases for 6 layers. We can pass them as arrays of pointers or a flattened buffer. Let's plan the kernel code structure. Weight storage: - For each layer l: - `W_ih_t[l]` shape `(input_size_l, 3*hidden_size)`. Input size for layer 0 is `input_size`, for others `hidden_size`. - `b_ih[l]` shape `(3*hidden)`. - `W_hh_t[l]` shape `(hidden_size, 3*hidden_size)`. - `b_hh[l]` shape `(3*hidden)`. We can allocate these as flattened contiguous arrays on the host and pass pointers. Implementation details: - Since `input_size` and `hidden_size` are dynamic, but for this problem they are fixed, we can pass them as template parameters or just regular arguments. - To reduce register pressure, we can process each time step and layer as described. Let's formalize the single-kernel approach. Grid: `(batch_size, 1)` blocks. blockDim.x = `hidden_size` = 256. But we need to handle hidden_size possibly not being exactly 256? The problem says hidden_size=256. We can set `blockDim.x = 256` and assume it matches. Or we can set `blockDim.x = min(256, hidden_size)`. Since hidden_size=256, it's fine. We also need to load x_t for layer 0 from global memory. For each t, all 256 threads cooperate to load x_t into `s_in` (128 floats). After that, __syncthreads. Then compute. For layers 1..5, the input to the layer is the previous layer's output. We can compute layer l, write output to `s_out[j]` (or overwrite `s_in`), then __syncthreads, and use `s_in` for next layer. So we only need one `s_in` buffer. But we must be careful: while computing layer l, we need to read `s_in[k]` for the dot product. Actually, during dot product, thread j reads `s_in[k]` for all k. No, we need to compute all `h_new` before overwriting `s_in` which is used as input. But the input to the layer is needed for all threads until their dot product is done. Since each thread only writes `s_in[j]`, and other threads read `s_in[k]` where k != j. If j != k, the write to `s_in[j]` doesn't affect thread k reading `s_in[k]`. However, what if a thread reads `s_in[k]` and another thread writes to `s_in[k]` simultaneously? Each thread writes to its own index j, so no two threads write to the same index. Thus, as long as threads don't read their own index `s_in[j]` after another thread has overwritten it (which won't happen because each thread only overwrites its own `s_in[j]` after reading all `s_in[k]` for k=0..hidden-1), it's safe! Thread j computes dot product of `W_ih_t[:, j]` with `s_in`. `s_in[j]` is just one of the k values. The thread reads `s_in[k]` for all k, including k=j. After finishing the dot product, the thread computes `h_new` and writes it to `s_h[l][j]`. For the next layer, we need the output of layer l as input. So after layer l, we can set `s_in[j] = s_h[l][j]` (or directly write to `s_in`). But we need a __syncthreads before the next layer starts, because next layer needs the full updated `s_in`. But that wastes shared memory. Since we have a barrier, we can do: ``` float h_new = ...; s_h[l][j] = h_new; if (l < num_layers - 1) { // after sync, s_in must contain h_new for all j // but we can't write s_in[j] = h_new before sync because other threads might still be reading s_in. // But they are reading s_in for the current layer (layer l). After each thread computes its h_new, // it's done reading s_in for this layer. So we can write s_in[j] = h_new after computing h_new, // as long as all threads have finished reading s_in. We can guarantee that if we compute h_new // and then __syncthreads(), because the sync ensures all threads finished reading s_in for this layer. // However, some threads might reach the write earlier than others, but as long as they have completed // their reads, writing to s_in[j] is fine. // Wait, but what about thread j reading s_in[k] and thread k writing to s_in[k] before thread j reads it? // Thread j's read of s_in[k] happens during the dot product, which is before computing h_new. // Thread k writes s_in[k] after computing h_new. If thread k finishes its dot product and h_new earlier than // thread j finishes its dot product, it might write to s_in[k] while thread j is still reading s_in[k]. // This is a race condition! So we cannot reuse s_in for input and output without double buffering or using s_h. } ``` So we need a separate buffer for the layer output before it's consumed by the next layer. We can use `s_h[l][j]` as the output. Then for the next layer, the input is `s_h[l-1][k]`? Wait, no: for layer l>0, input is output of layer l-1. But `s_h[l-1]` is the hidden state of layer l-1, which is the previous time step's hidden state! No! This is a critical confusion! Let's clarify: - `s_h[l]` stores the hidden state `h_t` for layer l, used for the recurrence within the same layer across time steps. - The input to layer l at time t is the output of layer l-1 at time t. - The output of layer l at time t is the new hidden state `h_t` for layer l (which is also the output if l is the last layer, or the input to layer l+1 if not). So at time t, for layer 0: - Input = x[t] - Hidden state = s_h[0] (from previous time step) - Compute new hidden state `new_h0`, write to `s_h[0]`. - This `new_h0` is also the input to layer 1. At time t, for layer 1: - Input = `new_h0` (output of layer 0 at time t). Not `s_h[0]` (which is hidden state for the recurrence in layer 0). Wait, actually for a standard RNN, the input to the next layer is the output of the current layer. For GRU, the output is the hidden state `h_t`. So yes, `new_h0` is both the hidden state for next step and the input to layer 1. Thus we need a separate buffer for the inter-layer input: let's call it `s_y[hidden]`. We compute layer 0, write `new_h0` to `s_y[j]`. Then for layer 1, input is `s_y[k]`. We compute `new_h1` from `s_y` and `s_h[1]`, write `new_h1` to `s_y[j]` (overwriting), and `s_h[1]` is updated. So `s_y` is a ping-pong buffer for the layer outputs. But can we reuse `s_y` safely with a single barrier? Yes: - At start of time t, `s_y` contains the input for layer 0 (x[t] loaded into first input_size). All threads read `s_y` during layer 0 computation. After computing `new_h0`, each thread writes `s_y[j] = new_h0`. But as before, thread j might write `s_y[j]` before thread k finishes reading `s_y[j]` for its dot product in layer 0. Race condition. So we need double buffering for `s_y`: `s_y[0]` and `s_y[1]`. For layer 0, input is from `s_y[read_buf]`. After computing, write to `s_y[write_buf]`. Then swap. For layer 1, input is from `s_y[read_buf]` (which contains layer 0 output). Compute, write to `s_y[write_buf]`. Swap, and so on. At the end of each layer, __syncthreads to ensure writes are done before next layer reads. Total shared memory: - `s_h`: `num_layers * hidden` floats = 6*256 = 1536 floats = 6KB. - `s_y[2]`: `2 * hidden` floats = 512 floats = 2KB. Total = 8KB. Still tiny. So the kernel has: ``` extern __shared__ float shared[]; float* s_h = shared; // size num_layers * hidden float* s_y0 = shared + num_layers * hidden; float* s_y1 = shared + num_layers * hidden + hidden; ``` Or we can allocate dynamically. Now, the weight loops: for layer l, we need `W_ih_t_l` and `W_hh_t_l`. We can pass arrays of pointers: `const float** W_ih_list`, etc. But CUDA doesn't easily handle arrays of device pointers in kernel arguments without copying to device first. Better to flatten: pack all layer weights into one big buffer, and pass offsets. Since layers have different input sizes, we can pack sequentially: - Layer 0: W_ih_t_0 size = input_size * 3*hidden = 128*768. - Layer 1-5: W_ih_t_l size = hidden * 3*hidden = 256*768. - For each layer, W_hh_t size = hidden * 3*hidden = 256*768. - Biases: each layer has b_ih and b_hh of size 3*hidden. We can precompute offsets and pass arrays of pointers via `const float* W_ih_list[6]` using a `const float**`. To pass a list of pointers to the kernel, we need to allocate a `float*` array on the device and copy pointers to it. That's extra overhead but negligible. Alternatively, we can pass 6 separate pointers for W_ih, 6 for W_hh, etc., since num_layers is at most 6 (from test) but could be variable. We can pass them as arrays of size `MAX_LAYERS` or as `std::vector` pointers. In CUDA C, we can use a helper struct or just pass individual pointers and loop through them using switch/if selecting pointer based on layer index. Since num_layers is variable but known at runtime, we could store them in a device constant array or simply pass as kernel arguments. In `load_inline`, the host wrapper can accept `std::vector<at::Tensor>` but `cpp_sources` must be C++ code. We can define a struct: ```cpp struct GRUParams { float* W_ih; float* b_ih; float* W_hh; float* b_hh; }; ``` Then pass `GRUParams* params` (device pointer) to the kernel. On host side, we allocate a `GRUParams` array on CPU, fill pointers, then copy to device via `cudaMalloc` + `cudaMemcpy`, and pass the device pointer. This is manageable. But we have to be careful: if num_layers is small (e.g., 3 in default), we only use first 3 entries. Now, within the kernel, for each layer l: - `const float* W_ih_t = params[l].W_ih;` - `const float* W_hh_t = params[l].W_hh;` - `int input_dim = (l == 0) ? input_size : hidden_size;` Then compute: ``` float acc_r, acc_z, acc_n; acc_r = params[l].b_ih[j]; acc_z = params[l].b_ih[j + hidden]; acc_n = params[l].b_ih[j + 2*hidden]; for (int k = 0; k < input_dim; ++k) { float xk = read_buf[k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden; acc_r += W_ih_t[base + j] * xk; acc_z += W_ih_t[base + j + hidden] * xk; acc_n += W_ih_t[base + j + 2*hidden] * xk; } float hr = params[l].b_hh[j]; float hz = params[l].b_hh[j + hidden]; float hn = params[l].b_hh[j + 2*hidden]; for (int k = 0; k < hidden; ++k) { float hk = s_h[l * hidden + k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden; hr += W_hh_t[base + j] * hk; hz += W_hh_t[base + j + hidden] * hk; hn += W_hh_t[base + j + 2*hidden] * hk; } float r = sigmoid(acc_r + hr); float z = sigmoid(acc_z + hz); float n = tanh(acc_n + r * hn); float h_new = (1.0f - z) * n + z * s_h[l * hidden + j]; s_h[l * hidden + j] = h_new; write_buf[j] = h_new; __syncthreads(); ``` After processing all layers, write `s_h[(num_layers-1)*hidden + j]` to `out[t*batch*hidden + b*hidden + j]`. Wait, we also need `h_n` for each layer. But the reference returns only output, not h_n. The PyTorch GRU returns h_n, but the reference model returns only output. So we don't need to compute h_n explicitly. However, `s_h[l]` at the final time step is h_n. Not needed. We need to be careful about `s_h` initial values: they come from `h0`. `h0` shape is `(num_layers, batch, hidden)`. For each block b, we load `h0[l*batch*hidden + b*hidden + j]` into `s_h[l*hidden + j]`. Also, `x` shape: if `batch_first=False`, x is `(seq, batch, input_size)`. The test code sets `batch_first=False`. The reference passes `x` as `(seq, batch, input)`. We need to handle both? The constructor has `batch_first`. The reference initializes `self.gru = nn.GRU(..., batch_first, ...)`. In the test code: `batch_first=False` (default). So x is `(seq, batch, input)`. But `ModelNew` must support `batch_first=True` as well to be general. We can check `self.batch_first` in Python and transpose if needed before calling the kernel. The custom kernel can assume `batch_first=False` (i.e., x is `(seq, batch, input)`). If `batch_first=True`, we can `x = x.transpose(0, 1)` in Python before calling the kernel. Similarly, the output should be transposed back if needed. This is cheap. Now, performance considerations inside the kernel: - Thread divergence: We need to load `x[t]` into `read_buf`. Since `input_size` may be less than `hidden_size` (128 vs 256), threads with `tid >= input_size` don't participate in loading. That's fine. We can have thread j read `x[t*batch*input + b*input + j]` if `j < input_size`. Or we can have all threads load in a loop. - Loops: unrolling. For `input_size=128` and `hidden_size=256`, loops are short. `#pragma unroll` might help if the sizes are compile-time constants. But they are parameters. We can pass them as template arguments if we generate kernel instances for the specific sizes. But the problem allows variable sizes; we can support runtime sizes and hope the compiler handles it. Alternatively, we can add `#pragma unroll 4` or similar for generic loops. - Use of Warp shuffle for reductions? Not needed, as each thread computes its own gate. - Activation functions: sigmoid and tanh. Use fast approximations? We should be careful: correctness tolerance is atol=rtol=1e-4 for fp32. Standard sigmoid/tanh are fine. We can implement `sigmoid(x) = 1 / (1 + expf(-x))` and `tanh(x)`. CUDA has `tanhf`. For speed, we might use `__expf` (intrinsic, less accurate) but it might be okay. However, 1e-4 tolerance might be borderline with `__expf`. Better to use `expf` and `tanhf` for safety. - `fmaf` could be used for better performance: `acc_r = fmaf(W, x, acc_r);`. The compiler usually converts to FMA automatically. We can use `fmaf` explicitly. Potential issue: The kernel uses many registers. Each thread has accumulators (acc_r, acc_z, acc_n, hr, hz, hn, h_new, r, z, n) plus loop variables. That's maybe 12 registers. Plus loading pointers. Plus shared memory indexing. Should be under 64 registers easily. So block of 256 threads uses maybe 8k registers, far under the 64k per SM limit. But the kernel has nested loops: outer loop `seq` (512), inner loops `num_layers` (6), and inside that loops over `input_dim` (up to 256) and `hidden` (256). Total iterations per thread: 512 * (128 + 256 + 6*(256 + ... wait, input_dim varies per layer). Actually per time step per layer: - Loop over input_dim: for layer 0: 128, others: 256. - Loop over hidden: 256. - Dot products: 3 per loop. Total per thread per layer: 3 * (input_dim + hidden) FMAs. Sum over layers: layer 0: 3*(128+256)=1152; layers 1-5: 5*3*(256+256)=7680; total per step: 8832 FMAs. Over 512 steps: 4.5M FMAs per thread. 256 threads per block: 1.15B FMAs per block. 10 blocks: 11.5B FMAs total. That's 23 GFLOPs, same as before. Instruction-level parallelism: each loop iteration has 3 independent FMAs (one for each gate). The compiler can schedule them. Loop unrolling by 4 would help. Can we optimize the input projection for layers > 0? For layers > 0, input_dim = hidden. So W_ih is the same shape as W_hh. But note: for layers 1..5, we could conceptually reuse the hidden projection? No, W_ih and W_hh are different weights. One more optimization: preload biases into registers or shared memory? Biases are small (3*hidden = 768 floats per layer). Could load them once per block into shared memory. But reading from global memory once per time step is also cheap (768 floats per layer per step, times 6 layers = 4608 floats per step, times 512 steps = 2.3M floats = 9MB). It's manageable. But we can do better: For each layer, load `b_ih[j]`, `b_ih[j+H]`, `b_ih[j+2H]` into registers at the start of the layer loop (outside the time loop). Similarly for `b_hh`. Since biases are constant per layer and j is fixed per thread, they stay in registers. That's good. For weights, we read from global memory inside the loops. Since the loops are long, loading the same weights across time steps is an L2 read. With 10 blocks, L2 can easily cache the weights. Wait, but each block reads the weights for its own batch element and its own j. For a given layer, W_ih_t is read by all blocks and all threads. Since total weight size per layer is ~0.75MB (W_ih + W_hh), and L2 is 50MB, all weights for all layers (~4.5MB) fit in L2. So weight reads are L2 hits after the first time. Great. So the kernel should be fast. Now, one more thing: the formula for PyTorch GRU when `bias=False`. We can handle it by conditionally adding biases. In the kernel, if bias pointer is NULL (or we pass `has_bias`), we just skip adding bias. But since biases are in registers, it's just a conditional addition. Simpler: in the Python wrapper, if `bias=False`, we pass tensors of zeros. No extra branching needed. But to avoid overhead, we can check `bias` in Python and pass zero tensors or actual biases. Now let's write the code. First, we need to extract weights and biases from PyTorch `nn.GRU` and store them in a format accessible by CUDA. Wait, `ModelNew` doesn't use `nn.GRU`. It needs to have its own weight parameters or we can extract weights in `__init__`. But the problem says: "Replace the forward computation of the PyTorch model below with a custom CUDA kernel. Your ModelNew class must: 1. Accept the same constructor arguments as Model." So `ModelNew.__init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers=3, bias=True, batch_first=False)` should create weights of the same shape as `nn.GRU`. We can create `nn.Parameter` for `weight_ih_l{k}`, `weight_hh_l{k}`, and biases if needed. Or we can just copy the weights from a reference `nn.GRU` module. Yes, this is clean. Let's verify pybind11 with `load_inline`: It should support `std::vector<at::Tensor>` as argument type. The auto-generated wrapper will use `py::cast` to convert Python list of tensors to `std::vector<at::Tensor>`. This is standard. So the C++ host function signature: ```cpp torch::Tensor gru_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_hh, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh, bool batch_first); ``` Inside the function: - Assert x is 3D, h0 is 3D. - If batch_first, x = x.transpose(0,1). We can do this in Python before calling C++. Let's do transpose in Python to keep C++ simple. - Determine `seq_len = x.size(0)`, `batch_size = x.size(1)`, `input_size = x.size(2)`. - `num_layers = h0.size(0)`. - `hidden_size = h0.size(2)`. - Create output tensor: `torch::empty({seq_len, batch_size, hidden_size}, x.options())`. - Create device params array: ```cpp std::vector<GRUParams> h_params(num_layers); for (int i=0; i<num_layers; i++) { h_params[i].W_ih = W_ih[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].b_ih = b_ih[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].W_hh = W_hh[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].b_hh = b_hh[i].data_ptr<float>(); } GRUParams* d_params; cudaMalloc(&d_params, num_layers * sizeof(GRUParams)); cudaMemcpy(d_params, h_params.data(), num_layers * sizeof(GRUParams), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice); ``` - Launch kernel: ```cpp dim3 block(hidden_size); dim3 grid(batch_size); size_t shared_mem = (num_layers + 2) * hidden_size * sizeof(float); gru_forward_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), seq_len, batch_size, input_size, out.data_ptr<float>(), h0.data_ptr<float>(), num_layers, hidden_size, d_params); ``` - `cudaFree(d_params);`. - Return output. Wait, `block = hidden_size`. If hidden_size=256, this works. But hidden_size could be larger than 1024? The test uses 256, so it's fine. But for safety, we can tile. However, to keep it simple and performing, we assume hidden_size <= 1024. We can add an assert. But we can make it more general: `int block_size = hidden_size;` if hidden_size > 1024, we can't have that many threads per block. We could throw an error. Let's just assert `hidden_size <= 1024`. This is reasonable for most GRUs. Now, the kernel code. ```cpp struct GRUParams { const float* W_ih_t; const float* b_ih; const float* W_hh_t; const float* b_hh; }; __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, int seq_len, int batch_size, int input_size, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ h0, int num_layers, int hidden_size, const GRUParams* __restrict__ params) { int b = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x; if (b >= batch_size || j >= hidden_size) return; extern __shared__ float shared[]; // layout: s_h[num_layers * hidden], s_y[2 * hidden] float* s_h = shared; float* s_y = shared + num_layers * hidden_size; float* s_h_read = s_y; // alias for first ping buffer? Let's manage manually. // Actually let's define: float* s_in = shared + num_layers * hidden_size; // size hidden float* s_out = s_in + hidden_size; // size hidden // Initialize hidden states from h0 for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) { s_h[l * hidden_size + j] = h0[l * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j]; } __syncthreads(); int read_buf = 0; // 0 means s_in, 1 means s_out? Wait we need two buffers. // Let's use s_in and s_out as two buffers. Initially load x into s_in. // After layer 0, write to s_out. After layer 1, write to s_in, etc. // We can just use two buffers and swap pointer. ``` Wait, using `s_in` and `s_out` shared arrays is good. But we need to load x into one buffer at each time step. Let's do this: ```cpp float* buf0 = shared + num_layers * hidden_size; float* buf1 = buf0 + hidden_size; float* read_buf = buf0; float* write_buf = buf1; for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { // Load x[t] for this batch into read_buf if (j < input_size) { read_buf[j] = x[t * batch_size * input_size + b * input_size + j]; } // For j >= input_size, we don't care, but to avoid uninitialized reads for layers >0, // we only read up to input_dim each layer. __syncthreads(); for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) { int input_dim = (l == 0) ? input_size : hidden_size; const GRUParams p = params[l]; float acc_r = p.b_ih[j]; float acc_z = p.b_ih[j + hidden_size]; float acc_n = p.b_ih[j + 2 * hidden_size]; // input projection for (int k = 0; k < input_dim; ++k) { float xk = read_buf[k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden_size; acc_r = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j], xk, acc_r); acc_z = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + hidden_size], xk, acc_z); acc_n = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], xk, acc_n); } float hr = p.b_hh[j]; float hz = p.b_hh[j + hidden_size]; float hn = p.b_hh[j + 2 * hidden_size]; // hidden projection for (int k = 0; k < hidden_size; ++k) { float hk = s_h[l * hidden_size + k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden_size; hr = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j], hk, hr); hz = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + hidden_size], hk, hz); hn = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], hk, hn); } float r = sigmoid(acc_r + hr); float z = sigmoid(acc_z + hz); float n = tanhf(acc_n + r * hn); float h_new = (1.0f - z) * n + z * s_h[l * hidden_size + j]; s_h[l * hidden_size + j] = h_new; write_buf[j] = h_new; __syncthreads(); // swap read and write buffers float* tmp = read_buf; read_buf = write_buf; write_buf = tmp; } // After all layers, the last layer's output is in s_h[(num_layers-1)*hidden + j]. // But note: after the last layer, we swapped buffers. The output was written to write_buf before swap. // Actually after layer l=num_layers-1, we did write_buf[j] = h_new; then __syncthreads(); then swap. // So read_buf now points to the buffer containing h_new (the last output). write_buf points to the other buffer. out[t * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j] = read_buf[j]; // For the next time step, we want the input to layer 0 to be x[t+1]. We'll load that into read_buf. // But note: currently read_buf contains the last layer output. We will overwrite it with x[t+1] at the top of the loop. // So no need to do anything else. __syncthreads(); // ensure read_buf is fully read before next iteration overwrites? Actually we write to read_buf at top. // Wait, at the top of next iteration, we load x[t+1] into read_buf[j]. But other threads might still be reading read_buf[j] from this step? // The last read of read_buf in this iteration was during the last layer. After that, we swapped and wrote output. // At the end of this iteration, we don't need read_buf anymore because next iteration overwrites it with x[t+1]. // But wait, the __syncthreads() at the start of next iteration ensures all threads have reached the end of this iteration. // However, thread j writes to read_buf[j] at the top of next iteration before other threads have read the old value? // There is no read of read_buf after the swap in the last layer, except the output write `out[...] = read_buf[j]`. // After that, there is a __syncthreads(). Then the next iteration starts. At the top of next iteration, thread j writes read_buf[j] = x.... // Since __syncthreads() ensures all threads have completed reading read_buf for output, it's safe to overwrite. // So the __syncthreads() at the end of the t-loop is not strictly necessary, but safe. } ``` Let's trace carefully: Initial: read_buf = buf0, write_buf = buf1. t loop start: 1. Load x[t] into read_buf (buf0). __syncthreads. 2. Layer 0: compute using read_buf (buf0). Write h_new to write_buf (buf1). __syncthreads. 3. Swap: read_buf=buf1, write_buf=buf0. 4. Layer 1: compute using read_buf (buf1). Write h_new to write_buf (buf0). __syncthreads. 5. Swap: read_buf=buf0, write_buf=buf1. 6. Layer 2: compute using read_buf (buf0). Write to write_buf (buf1). Swap -> read_buf=buf1. 7. ... After layer 5 (last layer): - Compute using read_buf (let's say buf0 or buf1 depending on parity). - Write h_new to write_buf. - __syncthreads. - Swap: read_buf = write_buf (now contains last output). - `out[...] = read_buf[j]`. - End of t loop. For next t: - read_buf currently contains last output. - But we load x[t+1] into read_buf, overwriting it. Is there any remaining read of the old read_buf? No. So it's safe after the output write is done for all threads. We have a __syncthreads at the end of t loop (or maybe not needed). Actually, at the very end of t loop, after output write, we should __syncthreads to ensure all threads have read read_buf[j] before next t overwrites. Wait, threads only read `read_buf[j]` for their own j when writing to `out`. There's no cross-thread read of read_buf[j] for j != own index? No, `out[...] = read_buf[j]` uses only the thread's own j. So no __syncthreads needed after that! Each thread is independent for that write. But we do need a __syncthreads at the start of the next t loop? Let's see: after layer l, we write `write_buf[j] = h_new`, then __syncthreads, then swap. After the last layer, we swapped and then write to out. No further reads of shared memory before the next t loop's load of x. The next t loop writes to `read_buf[j]`. But `read_buf` currently points to the buffer containing the last output. The next iteration's first action is loading x into `read_buf`. Since no other thread is reading `read_buf` after the output write, it's safe. However, to be safe, we can add a __syncthreads at the bottom of the t loop. It costs little. Wait, there is a subtle race: after `out[...] = read_buf[j]`, thread j proceeds to the bottom of the t loop. If there's no __syncthreads, thread 0 might finish the t loop and start the next t loop, writing to `read_buf[0]`, while thread 1 is still reading `read_buf[1]` for output. That's fine because they are different indices. No cross-thread read. So no barrier needed. However, after the bottom of the t loop, we go to the top. At the top, thread j writes `read_buf[j] = x[t+1]`. Could another thread still be reading `read_buf` from previous iteration? The last read of `read_buf` in previous iteration was in the last layer (before swap), or in `out[...] = read_buf[j]`. Both are in the same iteration. Since there's no shared read after that, no race. So we can remove the bottom __syncthreads. But let's keep it just to be safe. It's cost is trivial. Similarly, for layer l, after __syncthreads following write_buf write, we swap pointers. Then next layer reads read_buf. Since all threads wrote write_buf and synced, and swap is just pointer assignment (identical for all threads, no divergence), it's safe. One more thing: the initial load of `h0` into `s_h` needs a `__syncthreads` before starting time loop. I included that. Potential issue: `params` is in global memory, and we copy it to local memory in the layer loop: `const GRUParams p = params[l];`. This loads the struct (4 pointers + maybe padding) into registers. That's fine. Another issue: `read_buf[k]` for input projection. For layer 0, k goes up to `input_size` (128). For layers >0, k goes up to hidden (256). `read_buf` is sized to `hidden` floats. So for `k >= input_size`, we don't read (since loop bound is `input_dim`). For layer 0, threads with `j >= input_size` still participate in computation (they handle hidden units). They don't need to write `read_buf[j]` for layer 0. At the beginning of the t loop, we have: ``` if (j < input_size) read_buf[j] = x[...]; ``` Threads with j >= input_size skip this. The values in `read_buf[input_size..hidden-1]` are leftover from previous iteration's layer output. But they are never read for layer 0 because loop bound is input_size. So it's fine. But what if `j < input_size` writes to read_buf, and `j >= input_size` threads read read_buf[k] for k < input_size? That's fine because they are reading indices written by other threads. The `__syncthreads` after the load ensures all writes are visible. One potential optimization: use `float4` loads for x and weights? Since the accesses are coalesced, `float4` can reduce memory instructions. For x load: `if (j < input_size) read_buf[j] = x[...];` each thread loads one float. That's fine. For weights, each thread loads three floats per k. That's 3 loads. We could try vectorizing by processing 4 hidden units per thread. But then block size would be hidden/4 = 64. That might reduce register pressure and increase block count. But let's start simple. If performance is not enough, we can optimize. Let's think about the gemm-based cuDNN baseline. We don't know the exact time, but for seq=512, batch=10, hidden=256, 6 layers, cuDNN GRU might take around 0.2-0.5 ms. Triton kernels or custom kernels for small batch can sometimes beat cuDNN because cuDNN spends time on workspace, streams, etc. But our kernel is extremely lean: one launch, minimal memory traffic. I need to write the full Python file. Let's draft it. ### Python file structure ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # CUDA source cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> struct GRUParams { const float* W_ih_t; const float* b_ih; const float* W_hh_t; const float* b_hh; }; __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, int seq_len, int batch_size, int input_size, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ h0, int num_layers, int hidden_size, const GRUParams* __restrict__ params) { int b = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x; if (b >= batch_size || j >= hidden_size) return; extern __shared__ float shared[]; float* s_h = shared; float* s_buf0 = shared + num_layers * hidden_size; float* s_buf1 = s_buf0 + hidden_size; // init h0 for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) { s_h[l * hidden_size + j] = h0[l * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j]; } __syncthreads(); float* read_buf = s_buf0; float* write_buf = s_buf1; for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { if (j < input_size) { read_buf[j] = x[t * batch_size * input_size + b * input_size + j]; } __syncthreads(); for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) { int input_dim = (l == 0) ? input_size : hidden_size; const GRUParams p = params[l]; float acc_r = p.b_ih[j]; float acc_z = p.b_ih[j + hidden_size]; float acc_n = p.b_ih[j + 2 * hidden_size]; // input projection for (int k = 0; k < input_dim; ++k) { float xk = read_buf[k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden_size; acc_r = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j], xk, acc_r); acc_z = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + hidden_size], xk, acc_z); acc_n = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], xk, acc_n); } float hr = p.b_hh[j]; float hz = p.b_hh[j + hidden_size]; float hn = p.b_hh[j + 2 * hidden_size]; // hidden projection for (int k = 0; k < hidden_size; ++k) { float hk = s_h[l * hidden_size + k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden_size; hr = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j], hk, hr); hz = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + hidden_size], hk, hz); hn = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], hk, hn); } float r = sigmoid(acc_r + hr); float z = sigmoid(acc_z + hz); float n = tanhf(acc_n + r * hn); float h_new = (1.0f - z) * n + z * s_h[l * hidden_size + j]; s_h[l * hidden_size + j] = h_new; write_buf[j] = h_new; __syncthreads(); float* tmp = read_buf; read_buf = write_buf; write_buf = tmp; } // after all layers, read_buf contains the last layer output because of the final swap out[t * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j] = read_buf[j]; // no need for extra syncthreads here as next iteration starts with loading x into read_buf } } torch::Tensor gru_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_hh, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh) { // x: (seq, batch, input) // h0: (num_layers, batch, hidden) int seq_len = x.size(0); int batch_size = x.size(1); int input_size = x.size(2); int num_layers = h0.size(0); int hidden_size = h0.size(2); TORCH_CHECK(hidden_size <= 1024, "hidden_size must be <= 1024"); TORCH_CHECK(x.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "Only fp32 supported"); TORCH_CHECK(h0.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "Only fp32 supported"); auto out = torch::empty({seq_len, batch_size, hidden_size}, x.options()); std::vector<GRUParams> h_params(num_layers); for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { h_params[i].W_ih = W_ih[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].b_ih = b_ih[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].W_hh = W_hh[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].b_hh = b_hh[i].data_ptr<float>(); } GRUParams* d_params = nullptr; cudaMalloc(&d_params, num_layers * sizeof(GRUParams)); cudaMemcpy(d_params, h_params.data(), num_layers * sizeof(GRUParams), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice); dim3 block(hidden_size); dim3 grid(batch_size); size_t shared_mem_size = (num_layers + 2) * hidden_size * sizeof(float); gru_forward_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), seq_len, batch_size, input_size, out.data_ptr<float>(), h0.data_ptr<float>(), num_layers, hidden_size, d_params); cudaFree(d_params); return out; } """ cpp_source = """ torch::Tensor gru_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_hh, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh); """ gru_cuda = load_inline( name="gru_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["gru_forward_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers=3, bias=True, batch_first=False): super().__init__() self.input_size = input_size self.hidden_size = hidden_size self.num_layers = num_layers self.bias = bias self.batch_first = batch_first # Create parameters matching PyTorch GRU default initialization dummy = nn.GRU(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, bias, batch_first, dropout=0, bidirectional=False) for name, param in dummy.named_parameters(): self.register_parameter(name, nn.Parameter(param.data.clone())) del dummy def forward(self, x, h0): if self.batch_first: x = x.transpose(0, 1) seq_len, batch_size, input_size = x.shape num_layers = self.num_layers hidden_size = self.hidden_size # Gather weights W_ih = [] W_hh = [] b_ih = [] b_hh = [] for i in range(num_layers): W_ih.append(getattr(self, f'weight_ih_l{i}').t().contiguous()) W_hh.append(getattr(self, f'weight_hh_l{i}').t().contiguous()) if self.bias: b_ih.append(getattr(self, f'bias_ih_l{i}').contiguous()) b_hh.append(getattr(self, f'bias_hh_l{i}').contiguous()) else: b_ih.append(torch.zeros(3*hidden_size, dtype=x.dtype, device=x.device)) b_hh.append(torch.zeros(3*hidden_size, dtype=x.dtype, device=x.device)) out = gru_cuda.gru_forward_cuda(x, h0, W_ih, W_hh, b_ih, b_hh) if self.batch_first: out = out.transpose(0, 1) return out ``` Wait, there are several issues. 1. `has_bias`: if `bias=False`, PyTorch GRU doesn't have `bias_ih_l{i}` attributes. From PyTorch docs (as of recent versions): ``` r_t = σ(W_{ir} x_t + b_{ir} + W_{hr} h_{(t-1)} + b_{hr}) z_t = σ(W_{iz} x_t + b_{iz} + W_{hz} h_{(t-1)} + b_{hz}) n_t = tanh(W_{in} x_t + b_{in} + r_t * (W_{hn} h_{(t-1)} + b_{hn})) h_t = (1 - z_t) * n_t + z_t * h_{(t-1)} ``` This is exactly what we implemented. Good. Now, there is also `bidirectional=False`. We are not handling bidirectional. The problem says bidirectional=False. Good. What about dropout? The reference uses dropout=0. Good. Now, I need to check if my kernel has any race conditions on s_h when multiple layers. `s_h` is a 2D array indexed by `l*hidden + k`. Each layer l writes to `s_h[l*hidden + j]`. Different layers write to disjoint parts of `s_h`. But within a time step, after layer l writes to `s_h[l]`, the next layer l+1 reads `s_h[l+1]` (not `s_h[l]`). There is a `__syncthreads` after each layer. So no race. Wait, does layer l+1 read `s_h` for its own hidden state? Yes, `s_h[(l+1)*hidden + k]`. This was written at the previous time step (or initialized from h0). The current time step hasn't written to `s_h[(l+1)*hidden + k]` yet. So it's reading the old value, which is correct for recurrence. Thus, after we finish all layers for time t, `s_h[l]` contains the updated hidden state for all layers. Good. Now, the initial load of h0: we load all layers' h0. After that, `__syncthreads`. Good. One more thing: the bias tensors. If `bias=True`, PyTorch has two bias tensors per layer: `bias_ih_l{i}` and `bias_hh_l{i}`. If `bias=False`, no bias tensors. We handle it. But what about the naming? PyTorch state dict has `weight_ih_l0`, `bias_ih_l0` etc. We register them with `register_parameter(name, ...)`. Good. Now, let's also consider using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` with `cuda_sources`. Need to make sure the function signature matches. Let's write the code and compile it. I'll create a draft. Actually, compiling with `load_inline` might be slow, but it runs at module load time. The problem says we have limited tool calls. Using `compile_kernel` will compile and give errors. Let me write a first version and compile it with `compile_kernel`. Then check for correctness. Then optimize if needed. Wait, I need to pass the kernel code to `compile_kernel`. The argument is the full Python source. I should write the Python source as a string. Let me draft it. ```python kernel_code = r''' import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ torch::Tensor gru_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_hh, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh); """ cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <vector> struct GRUParams { const float* W_ih_t; const float* b_ih; const float* W_hh_t; const float* b_hh; }; __device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid(float x) { return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)); } __global__ void gru_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, int seq_len, int batch_size, int input_size, float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ h0, int num_layers, int hidden_size, const GRUParams* __restrict__ params) { int b = blockIdx.x; int j = threadIdx.x; if (b >= batch_size || j >= hidden_size) return; extern __shared__ float shared[]; float* s_h = shared; float* s_buf0 = shared + num_layers * hidden_size; float* s_buf1 = s_buf0 + hidden_size; // init h0 for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) { s_h[l * hidden_size + j] = h0[l * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j]; } __syncthreads(); float* read_buf = s_buf0; float* write_buf = s_buf1; for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) { if (j < input_size) { read_buf[j] = x[t * batch_size * input_size + b * input_size + j]; } __syncthreads(); for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) { int input_dim = (l == 0) ? input_size : hidden_size; GRUParams p = params[l]; float acc_r = p.b_ih[j]; float acc_z = p.b_ih[j + hidden_size]; float acc_n = p.b_ih[j + 2 * hidden_size]; for (int k = 0; k < input_dim; ++k) { float xk = read_buf[k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden_size; acc_r = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j], xk, acc_r); acc_z = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + hidden_size], xk, acc_z); acc_n = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], xk, acc_n); } float hr = p.b_hh[j]; float hz = p.b_hh[j + hidden_size]; float hn = p.b_hh[j + 2 * hidden_size]; for (int k = 0; k < hidden_size; ++k) { float hk = s_h[l * hidden_size + k]; int base = k * 3 * hidden_size; hr = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j], hk, hr); hz = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + hidden_size], hk, hz); hn = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], hk, hn); } float r = sigmoid(acc_r + hr); float z = sigmoid(acc_z + hz); float n = tanhf(acc_n + r * hn); float h_prev = s_h[l * hidden_size + j]; float h_new = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h_prev; s_h[l * hidden_size + j] = h_new; write_buf[j] = h_new; __syncthreads(); float* tmp = read_buf; read_buf = write_buf; write_buf = tmp; } out[t * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j] = read_buf[j]; } } torch::Tensor gru_forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor h0, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_hh, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_ih, std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh) { int seq_len = x.size(0); int batch_size = x.size(1); int input_size = x.size(2); int num_layers = h0.size(0); int hidden_size = h0.size(2); TORCH_CHECK(hidden_size <= 1024, "hidden_size must be <= 1024"); TORCH_CHECK(x.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "fp32 only"); TORCH_CHECK(h0.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "fp32 only"); auto out = torch::empty({seq_len, batch_size, hidden_size}, x.options()); std::vector<GRUParams> h_params(num_layers); for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { h_params[i].W_ih = W_ih[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].b_ih = b_ih[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].W_hh = W_hh[i].data_ptr<float>(); h_params[i].b_hh = b_hh[i].data_ptr<float>(); } GRUParams* d_params = nullptr; cudaMalloc(&d_params, num_layers * sizeof(GRUParams)); cudaMemcpy(d_params, h_params.data(), num_layers * sizeof(GRUParams), cudaMemcpyHostToDevice); dim3 block(hidden_size); dim3 grid(batch_size); size_t shared_mem = (num_layers + 2) * hidden_size * sizeof(float); gru_forward_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), seq_len, batch_size, input_size, out.data_ptr<float>(), h0.data_ptr<float>(), num_layers, hidden_size, d_params); cudaFree(d_params); return out; } """ gru_cuda = load_inline( name="gru_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["gru_forward_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, input_size, hidden_size, num_layers=3, bias=True, batch_first=False): super().__init__() self.input_size = input_size self.hidden_size = hidden_size self.num_layers = num_layers self.bias = bias self.batch_first = batch_first dummy = nn.GRU(input_size, hidden_size, num_layers, bias, batch_first, dropout=0, bidirectional=False) for name, param in dummy.named_parameters(): self.register_parameter(name, nn.Parameter(param.data.clone())) del dummy def forward(self, x, h0): if self.batch_first: x = x.transpose(0, 1) num_layers = self.num_layers hidden_size = self.hidden_size device = x.device W_ih = [] W_hh = [] b_ih = [] b_hh = [] for i in range(num_layers): W_ih.append(getattr(self, f'weight_ih_l{i}').t().contiguous()) W_hh.append(getattr(self, f'weight_hh_l{i}').t().contiguous()) if self.bias: b_ih.append(getattr(self, f'bias_ih_l{i}').contiguous()) b_hh.append(getattr(self, f'bias_hh_l{i}').contiguous()) else: b_ih.append(torch.zeros(3 * hidden_size, dtype=torch.float32, device=device)) b_hh.append(torch.zeros(3 * hidden_size, dtype=torch.float32, device=device)) out = gru_cuda.gru_forward_cuda(x, h0, W_ih, W_hh, b_ih, b_hh) if self.batch_first: out = out.transpose(0, 1) return out ''' ``` I need to be careful with `cpp_sources`: it should include `#include <vector>` maybe. `torch/extension.h` might include it, but let's be safe. Also, we need to declare `std::vector<torch::Tensor>`. Actually, the function is defined in the `.cu` file. Does `cuda_source` need to include `cpp_source` declarations? No, `cpp_source` is compiled separately as C++. The linker links them. So the definition in `.cu` must match declaration in `.cpp`. But there is a catch: in some `load_inline` versions, the `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` are compiled into separate translation units. So the function in `.cu` should not be `static`. It's fine. One more thing: the kernel uses `GRUParams` which is defined in `.cu`. The host function `gru_forward_cuda` also uses it. The `cpp_source` only has the declaration of `gru_forward_cuda`. That's fine. Now compile it.

std::vector<torch::Tensor> b_hh);\n\"\"\"\n\ncuda_source = r\"\"\"\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <vector>\n\nstruct GRUParams {\n const float* W_ih_t;\n const float* b_ih;\n const float* W_hh_t;\n const float* b_hh;\n};\n\n__device__ __forceinline__ float sigmoid(float x) {\n return 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x));\n}\n\n__global__ void gru_forward_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ x,\n int seq_len,\n int batch_size,\n int input_size,\n float* __restrict__ out,\n const float* __restrict__ h0,\n int num_layers,\n int hidden_size,\n const GRUParams* __restrict__ params)\n{\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int j = threadIdx.x;\n if (b >= batch_size || j >= hidden_size) return;\n\n extern __shared__ float sdata[];\n float* sh = sdata; // num_layers * hidden_size\n float* sbuf0 = sh + num_layers * hidden_size; // hidden_size\n float* sbuf1 = sbuf0 + hidden_size; // hidden_size\n\n // Load initial hidden states\n for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) {\n sh[l * hidden_size + j] = h0[l * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n float* read_buf = sbuf0;\n float* write_buf = sbuf1;\n\n for (int t = 0; t < seq_len; ++t) {\n // Load input for layer 0 into read_buf\n if (j < input_size) {\n read_buf[j] = x[t * batch_size * input_size + b * input_size + j];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) {\n int in_dim = (l == 0) ? input_size : hidden_size;\n GRUParams p = params[l];\n\n // Linear terms from x/h input\n float acc_r = p.b_ih[j];\n float acc_z = p.b_ih[j + hidden_size];\n float acc_n = p.b_ih[j + 2 * hidden_size];\n\n for (int k = 0; k < in_dim; ++k) {\n float xk = read_buf[k];\n int base = k * 3 * hidden_size;\n acc_r = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j], xk, acc_r);\n acc_z = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + hidden_size], xk, acc_z);\n acc_n = fmaf(p.W_ih_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], xk, acc_n);\n }\n\n // Linear terms from previous hidden state\n float hr = p.b_hh[j];\n float hz = p.b_hh[j + hidden_size];\n float hn = p.b_hh[j + 2 * hidden_size];\n\n for (int k = 0; k < hidden_size; ++k) {\n float hk = sh[l * hidden_size + k];\n int base = k * 3 * hidden_size;\n hr = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j], hk, hr);\n hz = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + hidden_size], hk, hz);\n hn = fmaf(p.W_hh_t[base + j + 2 * hidden_size], hk, hn);\n }\n\n float r = sigmoid(acc_r + hr);\n float z = sigmoid(acc_z + hz);\n float n = tanhf(acc_n + r * hn);\n\n float h_prev = sh[l * hidden_size + j];\n float h_new = (1.0f - z) * n + z * h_prev;\n sh[l * hidden_size + j] = h_new;\n write_buf[j] = h_new;\n __syncthreads();\n\n float* tmp = read_buf; read_buf = write_buf; write_buf = tmp;\n }\n\n // After finishing all layers, read_buf holds the output of the last layer\n out[t * batch_size * hidden_size + b * hidden_size + j] = read_buf[j];\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor gru_forward_cuda(\n torch::Tensor x,\n torch::Tensor h0,\n std::vector<torch::Tensor> W_ih,\n std::vector<torch::Tensor> Could there be an issue where a thread reads from `sh[l * hidden_size + k]` while another thread in a different layer is writing to it? No, because `__syncthreads()` ensures no thread proceeds until all have finished writing. Wait! I think I might see a potential issue. In the loop: ```cpp for (int l = 0; l < num_layers; ++l) { ... sh[l * hidden_size + j] = h_new; write_buf[j] = h_new; __syncthreads(); float* tmp = read_buf; read_buf = write_buf; write_buf = tmp; } ``` After `__syncthreads()`, all threads swap their local pointers. But then the next iteration starts immediately. No additional `__syncthreads()` is needed between layers because by the time any thread reads `read_buf[k]` in the next layer, the previous `__syncthreads()` has already ensured all writes to `write_buf` (which is now `read_buf`) are visible to all threads in the block. The shared memory itself doesn't change, only the pointers change. And all threads can read from shared memory that was written before the `__syncthreads()`. So no race. For a single layer, processing timestep-by-timestep vs the same recurrence gives the same result (same associativity order for the dot products). But for multi-layer, doing timestep-by-timestep (process t=0 for all layers, then t=1 for all layers) vs layer-by-layer (process all t for layer 0, then all t for layer 1) should give bitwise identical results because each layer is a deterministic function of its inputs and previous hidden states. For the SAME layer, processing all timesteps in order gives the SAME hidden states as processing them one by one (the recurrence is serial within a layer). So the outputs should be identical. The total FLOPs and mathematical result are the same. In my kernel, I do `acc = b` then loop and accumulate `acc += W[k] * x[k]`. This is element-wise. The difference is that GEMM might accumulate in a different order (e.g., tiling, parallel reduction), leading to different rounding. For a dot product of two vectors of length `input_size`, different accumulation orders can differ by ~`input_size * epsilon`. For input_size=128, epsilon ~ 1e-7, so difference ~ 1e-5. This is exactly the average difference we see! And for some elements, the difference might be larger due to cancellation or because the sigmoid amplifies small differences near the origin. Actually, the sigmoid amplifies differences most near its steepest point (x=0), where the derivative is 0.25. So a difference of 1e-5 in the linear term becomes ~2.5e-6 in the sigmoid. That's still tiny. But tanh amplifies near x=0 with derivative 1. So a difference of 1e-5 becomes 1e-5. And then (1-z)*n + z*h: z is a sigmoid output (≈0.5), n is tanh output (≈0). The combination could amplify depending on the specific values. Still, the maximum would typically be a few multiples of the average. 0.05 / 3e-5 ≈ 1667x. That seems really large. Is it possible for two dot product accumulation orders to differ by 0.2? For 128 elements and values in [0,1], the dot product is a sum of 128 products. The rounding error in a naive summation is about `128 * epsilon * typical_product`. If typical values are ~0.1, products are ~0.01, and error is ~128 * 1e-7 * 0.01 = 1.6e-7. Very small. But if we consider that the weights can be positive or negative, and cancellation can occur... even so, the error bound is small. Wait, I might be thinking about this wrong. The GEMM in PyTorch uses multiple threads/accumulators. For a single output element, the dot product is split into tiles, each tile is partially summed in parallel, and then the partial sums are added. If input_size=128 and hidden_size=256, the GEMM might use 128-way parallelism for each output element. The partial sums could all be ~O(1), and when summed together, the rounding error could be much larger. Actually, I just realized something important. PyTorch on H100 with cuDNN likely uses TF32 GEMM. TF32 has 10 bits of mantissa. FP32 has 23 bits. The relative error of TF32 is about 2^-10 ≈ 1e-3. For dot products, the absolute error could be ~1e-3 * product_magnitude * sqrt(N) or something. With N=128, errors could easily reach 0.01, and with sigmoid/tanh amplification, output differences could reach 0.05! So the difference might actually be TF32 vs FP32!
